                .build()

        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202404442')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e4256f273d2e4077b9980ea3a3ea8215">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1b16439d-9c23-438a-9541-4056f435845f'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d21364381" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> with tunnel structure is considered a promising low-cost cathode material for sodium-ion batteries. However, the sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics and low initial Coulombic efficiency restrict its practical applications in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries. Herein, a manganese-based tunnel-structured cathode with high rate capability and high initial Coulombic efficiency is prepared by niobium doping and sodium compensation. Via materials characterizations and theoretical calculations, it is demonstrated that a proper amount of niobium doping in tunnel structure can effectively improve its structural stability and charge transport kinetics, resulting in outstanding rate capability (76.6% capacity retained from 0.5 to 30&nbsp;C) and superior cycling performance (82.3% capacity retention after 800 cycles at 5&nbsp;C) for the optimized Nb-doped Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode (Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.98</sub>Nb<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub>). Furthermore, NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is added into the Na<sub>0.44</sub>Mn<sub>0.98</sub>Nb<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode as a self-sacrificing sodium compensation additive, and a high initial Coulombic efficiency close to 100% is achieved for the composite cathode. This work establishes a facile strategy to design advanced manganese-based cathode materials for large-scale energy storage applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404442-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Owing to the low cost and rich abundance of sodium resources, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered the most suitable technological complement to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for electric vehicles and renewable energy storage in smart grids.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0001_R_d21364373e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0002_R_d21364373e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - J. Power Sources">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the commercialization of SIBs is still hindered by their inferior electrochemical performance to LIBs, which demands the development of high-performance cathode and anode materials. In recent years, many interesting materials including Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0003_R_d21364373e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0004_R_d21364373e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0005_R_d21364373e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Mater. Chem. A">5</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0007_R_d21364373e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Rev.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> organic compounds,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0008_R_d21364373e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0010_R_d21364373e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Mater. Today">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and layered transition metal oxides<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0011_R_d21364373e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Mater. Chem. A">11</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0014_R_d21364373e511" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Nat. Commun.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been extensively explored as cathode materials for SIBs. Among them, the Mn-based cathode materials have attracted particular attention due to their low cost, abundant manganese resource, and environmental benignity. Typically, the layered manganese-based cathode materials, such as O3 and P2, suffer from poor structural stability, which causes severe structural deterioration and poor cyclability upon cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0015_R_d21364373e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0017_R_d21364373e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, tunnel-structured Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> possesses good structural stability during repeated electrochemical sodium (de)intercalation reactions, due to its unique charge ordering and tunnel structure framework.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0018_R_d21364373e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">18</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0021_R_d21364373e526" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - J. Power Sources">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The structural frame of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> is built up of double and triple rutile-type chains with edge-sharing MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and single chains with corner-sharing MnO<sub>5</sub> square pyramids, resulting in the large S-shaped tunnels and small tunnels occupied by Na<sup>+</sup> ions. The Na<sup>+</sup> ions located in the S-shaped tunnels can be reversibly extracted and inserted with the Na/Mn molar ratio varying between 0.22 and 0.66, which results in a poor initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) (&lt;50%). By far, Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> still suffers from unsatisfactory electrochemical performance, especially the poor rate performance and low ICE, which greatly limits their practical application in SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Intense efforts have been devoted to improving the electrochemical performance of tunnel-structured manganese-based cathode materials for SIBs. For instance, foreign ion doping (e.g. Co<sup>3+</sup>, F<sup>−</sup>， Ti<sup>4+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>, and Mg<sup>2+</sup>)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0015_R_d21364373e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0017_R_d21364373e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0022_R_d21364373e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Stor. Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0024_R_d21364373e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Electrochem. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> in Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> has been proven to be an effective strategy to mitigate fast structural degradation and promote electrode kinetics. Considering the poor ICE of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in SIBs, exploring the self-sacrificial additives could be a facile method to compensate for the initial sodium deficiency. Previous studies revealed that sacrificing sodium additives including NaN<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0023_R_d21364373e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Power Sources">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0024_R_d21364373e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Electrochem. Commun.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>3</sub>P,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0025_R_d21364373e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> NaNO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0026_R_d21364373e594" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>NiO<sub>2</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0027_R_d21364373e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0028_R_d21364373e613" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>2</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0029_R_d21364373e624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - ChemSusChem">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> can provide extra sodium ions to the anode during the first charge process, thus elevating ICE to nearly 100%. By far, these strategies are not well integrated in tunnel-structured manganese-based cathodes, which limits their further development and practical application in SIBs. Combining high ICE, good rate performance, and long cycle life, the tunnel-type Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> could be an ideal low-cost cathode material for SIBs, which has not yet been successfully developed.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we proposed niobium-doped Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> as a potential cathode material for nonaqueous SIBs with ultrastable and ultrafast Na<sup>+</sup> storage, which was synthesized via a simple solid-state reaction method. Through structural characterizations and density functional theory (DFT) calculations, it has been disclosed that the introduction of niobium (Nb) ions can simultaneously reinforce the tunnel structure and enable fast electron and Na<sup>+</sup> transport, thus resulting in greatly improved cycle life and rate capability. To increase the ICE of Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> in SIBs, NaCrO<sub>2</sub>, as a sacrificial Na additive, has been applied to compensate for the deficient 0.22 Na<sup>+</sup> in the first charge of the optimized Nb-doped Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> cathode, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0001">1a</a>. With the rational structural modification and Na compensation, we demonstrated that the tunnel-structured manganese-based cathodes are promising for practical application in SIBs.</p>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404442-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404442-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural Characterizations of Nb-Doped Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub></h3>
            
            <p>Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> and Nb-doped Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> with different Nb/Mn molar ratios of 0.005:1, 0.01:1, 0.02:1, and 0.03:1 were prepared and denoted as NMO, NMO-0.5, NMO-1, NMO-2, and NMO-3 respectively, as described in details in Experimental Section. To identify the effect of Nb-doping on the crystal structure modification, X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of these five samples were measured in a 2Theta range of 10–70 degrees. As displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), all diffraction peaks of the five samples can be indexed to the typical tunnel-type NMO structure (JCPDS card no. 27–0750) without impurity phase, suggesting that Nb has been successfully doped into the lattice of the tunnel-type NMO. It can be observed that diffraction peaks first shift to lower angles monotonously with the increase of Nb content (from 0% to 2%) and then shift backward for a higher Nb content (3%), thus the corresponding evolution of d-spacing can be evaluated according to the Bragg equation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0030_R_d21364373e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Chem. Educ.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To verify whether the absence of impurity phase in the Nb-doped samples is related to the low concentrations of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> dopant, the XRD pattern of NMO mixed with 2 wt% Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> was displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). The XRD pattern revealed the characteristic diffraction peaks of Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, demonstrating the capability of XRD to directly detect impurity phases. These results further confirm the phase purity of the Nb-doped NMO samples. Furthermore, inductively coupled plasma optical emission spectrometry (ICP-OES) measurements were conducted and the results are presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). The atomic ratios of Nb: Mn for the synthesized NMO-2 and NMO-3 samples were determined to be 0.018:1 and 0.029:1, closely aligning with the precursor values of 0.02 and 0.03, respectively. Additionally, it is observed that the atomic ratios of Na: Mn for the NMO-3 (0.431:1) was smaller than those of NMO and NMO-2 (0.439:1 and 0.442:1). The reduced Na content (Na vacancy formation) in NMO-3 could lead to lattice shrinkage, which is consistent with the aforementioned XRD results. The XRD Rietveld refinement profiles of NMO and NMO-2 are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0001">1b,c</a>. All the diffraction peaks of NMO and NMO-2 samples are well indexed into an orthorhombic structure with the <i>Pbam</i> space group. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0001">1d,e</a>, the framework of the tunnel structure is constructed by double and triple rutile-type chains with edge-shared MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and single chains of corner-shared MnO<sub>5</sub> square-pyramids, in which Mn has five crystallographic occupation sites. The Mn1, Mn2, and Mn5 sites are occupied by Mn<sup>4+</sup>, while Mn3 and Mn4 are occupied by Mn<sup>3+</sup>. Two types of tunnels are arranged to form the structural framework: large S-shaped tunnels with mobile Na<sup>+</sup> ions and small tunnels with fixed Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Lattice parameters and volume variations of different samples derived from XRD Rietveld refinement are summarized in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, all the <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> lattice parameters (<i>a</i> = 9.111, <i>b</i> = 26.480, and <i>c</i> = 2.831&nbsp;Å) for NMO-2 show expansion as compared to those of NMO (<i>a</i> = 9.094, <i>b</i> = 26.476, and <i>c</i> = 2.826&nbsp;Å). Accordingly, volume expansion was found in the unit cell of NMO-2 structure (682.773&nbsp;Å<sup>3</sup> vs. 680.525&nbsp;Å<sup>3</sup>), which could lead to the expansion of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels. Thus, the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation kinetics in tunnel structure could be enhanced by proper Nb doping. To verify the evolution in the oxidation states of Mn and Nb, X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were performed on the NMO and NMO-2 samples. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information) displays the survey-scan XPS spectrum of the NMO-2 sample, confirming the presence of all expected elements. Moreover, the semiquantitative atomic ratio of Nb to Mn was determined to be 0.0187, which is in consistent with the ICP results. Additionally, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0001">1f</a> shows the Nb 2d core-level XPS spectrum, and the two peaks centered at ≈209.4 and 206.7&nbsp;eV can be attributed to Nb 2d<sub>3/2</sub> and 2d<sub>5/2</sub>, indicating Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping in the NMO-2 sample. The Mn 3s core-level XPS spectra of NMO and NMO-2 are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0001">1g</a>. The splitting width of the two Mn 3s peaks for the NMO sample is 4.88&nbsp;eV, indicating the coexistence of Mn<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>4+</sup> ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0031_R_d21364373e816" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is noted that the NMO-2 sample exhibits larger peak splitting width (4.93&nbsp;eV) than that of the NMO sample, suggesting Nb doping reduces the average valence state of Mn. The Mn 2p core-level XPS spectra were further recorded to investigate the oxidation state of Mn, displaying two peaks of Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> at ≈653.5 and 641.8&nbsp;eV, respectively, with a spin-energy separation of ≈11.7&nbsp;eV. Moreover, each peak can be deconvoluted into two components assigning to the Mn<sup>3+</sup> at 641.5 and 653.2&nbsp;eV and Mn<sup>4+</sup> at 642.6 and 654.3&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0032_R_d21364373e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0033_R_d21364373e830" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Particularly, it is worth noting that the semiquantitative ratio of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> in NMO-2 sample (0.84) is higher than that of the NMO sample (0.76), while the theoretical ratio of Mn<sup>3+</sup>/Mn<sup>4+</sup> is equal to 0.44:0.56 (≈0.79) for the NMO sample,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0034_R_d21364373e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Energy Mater.">34</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0035_R_d21364373e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Power Sources">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting that partial Mn<sup>4+</sup> is successfully substituted by Nb<sup>5+</sup> in NMO-2.</p>
            
            <p>The morphology and microstructure of the samples were characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The SEM images of the as-synthesized samples with different Nb doping levels display a rod-shaped morphology for all particles with ≈0.2–1&nbsp;µm in width and ≈2–7&nbsp;µm in length (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The fusion of some particles can be observed owing to the high calcination temperature (900&nbsp;°C). TEM images (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2a,d</a>) further reveal the oriented nanorod-like particles for NMO and NMO-2 samples. The energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2g</a>) attest to the uniform distribution of Na, Mn, O, and Nb elements throughout the nanorod particles of the NMO-2 sample. Based on the XRD and TEM results, it is speculated that Nb is homogeneously distributed and introduced into the tunnel structure without impurity phase formation. The high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) and corresponding FFT images are provided in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2b,e</a>. The well-defined lattice fringes from HRTEM images illustrate a high degree of crystallinity of the obtained NMO and NMO-2 samples. Meanwhile, the interplanar spacing of 0.291 nm ascribed to the (001) planes of the NMO-2 tunnel structure is larger than 0.282&nbsp;nm of NMO, agreeing well with the expanded lattice parameters with Nb doping in XRD results. The corresponding Fast Fourier Transform (FFT) patterns present well-resolved spots, corroborating the highly crystallized tunnel structure. Note that the FFT image of the NMO-2 sample (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2e</a>) recorded along the [010] direction displays a spot matrix of the tunnel-type NMO with (200) and (001) crystal planes, indicating that the nanorods tend to grow along the [001] direction, which is consistence with the previous reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0036_R_d21364373e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">36</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0038_R_d21364373e875" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The variation in interplanar spacing of (001) planes between NMO and NMO-2 samples was accurately probed with the line-scan intensity profiles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2c</a>). It is visibly depicted that the interplanar spacing between adjacent Mn/Nb layers in NMO-2 is larger than that of adjacent Mn planes in NMO. Such phenomenon is induced by large-sized Nb<sup>5+</sup> (0.064&nbsp;Å) riveting in the Mn<sup>4+</sup> (0.053&nbsp;Å) layer, resulting in the expansion of the adjacent Mn/Nb layers of (001) planes as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2f</a>, which agrees well with the XRD results.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404442-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6bc7cb44-a909-48bd-b11f-27da3b54b1d3/adfm202404442-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6bc7cb44-a909-48bd-b11f-27da3b54b1d3/adfm202404442-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f45b002f-c1af-4644-aad8-1fa92daa06f1/adfm202404442-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6bc7cb44-a909-48bd-b11f-27da3b54b1d3/adfm202404442-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404442-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404442" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">&nbsp;a and d) TEM images, b and e) HRTEM images (insets are the corresponding FFT patterns) of NMO and NMO-2 samples. c) Line-scaning intensity profiles corresponding to the green line in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2b</a> and the red line in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0002">2e</a>. f) Schematic illustration of the expanded (001) crystal planes of NMO-2 sample with Nb doping. g) EDS elemental mapping images of the NMO-2 nanorod.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404442-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium Storage Performance</h3>
            
            <p>Sodium storage performance of the as-synthesized samples as cathodes was evaluated in half cells to explore the influence of Nb doping. <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0003">3a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a> (Supporting Information) display the initial charge/discharge profiles of the NMO, NMO-0.5, NMO-1, NMO-2, and NMO-3 cathodes at a current rate of C/10 (1 C = 100&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). The five cathodes present similar voltage profiles in shape, which indicate the same reaction mechanism for charge and discharge. With the increase of Nb content, the initial charge-specific capacities are 61.1, 58.9, 58.8, 58.5, and 58.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and the discharge-specific capacities are 119.3, 118.5, 117.6, 116.3 and 115.5&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, yielding the poor ICE of ≈50.0%. Multiple plateaus were observed during Na extraction/insertion for the five cathodes, which is associated with Na<sup>+</sup> vacancy ordering and/or charge ordering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0039_R_d21364373e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nat. Commun.">39</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0041_R_d21364373e944" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Inorg. Chem.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the specific capacities slightly degenerate with the increase of Nb content, which can be attributed to the substitution of electrochemically active Mn ions by electrochemical inactive Nb ions. The different cathodes were further charged and discharged at different current rates from 0.5 C (50&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) to 30 C (3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and the recorded charge/discharge profiles are shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0003">3b,c</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information). The specific discharge capacities of the NMO cathode were found to be 107.1, 98.9, 91.3, 82.4, 75.4, 69.1 and 60.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at constant current rates of 0.5, 2, 5, 10, 15, 20 and 30 C, respectively. In contrast, the Nb-doped cathodes present higher capacity retention at the same current rate and smaller polarization with increasing current density. Particularly, the NMO-2 cathode delivered discharge capacities of 106.6, 102.2, 98.4, 93.2, 89.7, 86.6 and 81.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at constant current rates of 0.5, 2, 5, 10, 15, 20 and 30 C, respectively. It is especially important that the multi-platform characteristics of Nb-doped cathodes were maintained even when the current rate increases to 30 C compared with that of NMO sample. Therefore, the Nb-doped cathodes exhibited enhanced rate capabilities as illustrated in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0003">3d</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5b</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, when the current rate goes back to 0.5 C, the discharge capacity of the NMO-2 cathode can be fully recovered to the initial value (106.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), implying its superior reversibility. Specially, the capacity retention of the NMO-2 cathode at 30&nbsp;C is 76.6%, which is significantly improved compared with unmodified NMO cathode (56.1%). The enhancement in rate performance can be attributed to the enlarged lattice parameters introduced by Nb doping in the tunnel structure, which favors accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. To evaluate the Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics of the as prepared samples, cyclic voltammogram (CV) measurements with a stepwise scanning rate of 0.1–1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> were carried out and the obtained CV curves are shown in Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0003">3e,f</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information). The Randles–Sevcik relation was calculated to evaluate the chemical diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> in NMO and NMO-2 samples, based on the following equation<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0042_R_d21364373e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Electrochim. Acta">42</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0043_R_d21364373e988" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Mater. Chem. A">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404442-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0d2c9dd4-810c-45ba-820a-faa435f14a09/adfm202404442-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>

As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0003">3g</a>, the straight line of the NMO-2 cathode presents a steeper slope than the NMO cathode, indicating faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in NMO-2. In particular, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) for NMO-2 was calculated to be ≈6.58&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than that of NMO (3.72&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), indicating that Nb doping can promote ion-diffusion dynamics of NMO cathode. To further investigate the benefit of Nb doping for electrode kinetics, electrochemical impedence spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were carried out for different electrodes. The Nyquist plots of the five different electrodes are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7d</a> (Supporting Information), which were fitted by employing an equivalent circuit inserted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7d</a> (Supporting Information). Here, <i>R<sub>s</sub></i> associated to the high frequency intercept at <i>Z</i><sup>׳</sup> axis is the ohmic resistance of the cell components; <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> donotes the charge transfer resistance correlating to the semicircle in the high-to-medium frequency region; <i>Z<sub>W</sub></i> determined by the sloping line in the low frequency region denotes the Warburg impedance correlating to ion migration in cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0044_R_d21364373e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Sci.">44</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0046_R_d21364373e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Energy Environ. Sci.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is illustrated that the EIS spectra of all Nb-doped NMO electrodes present smaller <i>R<sub>ct</sub></i> and larger slope for the straight line than those of NMO. Moreover, the suggested appropriate Nb doping (NMO-2) can simultaneously enhance the electron transport and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion in NMO. The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) measurements were further employed to assess the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics of NMO and NMO-2 cathodes. The sodium diffusion coefficient (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9e5c8875-bfe9-4ab4-9269-7a475b0684d8/adfm202404442-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404442:adfm202404442-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202404442-math-0002.png">

Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a,b</a> (Supporting Information) display the GITT curves of both NMO and NMO-2 cathodes during the initial charge and discharge at 0.5 C. The observed potential variation during each relaxation stage serves as an indicator of over-potential. In comparison to NMO, the NMO-2 cathode exhibits reduced over-potential, indicating decreased electrochemical polarization. Moreover, the as calculated Na<sup>+</sup> chemical diffusion coefficients (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/63f7ab77-6808-4149-9cfa-4e4680d088f6/adfm202404442-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404442:adfm202404442-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adfm202404442-math-0004.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <msubsup>
                        <mi>D</mi>
                        <mi>Na</mi>
                        <mo>+</mo>
                     </msubsup>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$D_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^ + $</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8c</a>, Supporting Information) of NMO-2 range from 4.23&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup> to 1.18&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−9</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, which are significantly higher than those of NMO (3.22&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−11</sup> to 1.27&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−10</sup>&nbsp;cm<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>), demonstrating that Nb introduction in tunnel structure can accelerate Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics. The long-term cycle performances of the as-synthesized samples at 5 C are further compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0003">3h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c</a> (Supporting Information). Among the tested samples, the NMO-2 cathode exhibited superior cycling stability and achieved 82.3% capacity retention after 800 cycles, whereas the capacity retentions for NMO, NMO-0.5, NMO-1 and NMO-3 are 57.9%, 62.1%, 72.9% and 60.2%, respectively. Moreover, when tested at 1 C, the NMO-2 cathode displays superior cycle performance with 90.7% capacity retained after 200 cycles, while the NMO cathode experiences faster capacity fading with ≈82.5% capacity retained after 200 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). As compared in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), the NMO-2 cathode displays superior rate performance and cycling stability to the previously reported NMO-based cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0017_R_d21364373e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0022_R_d21364373e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Stor. Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0035_R_d21364373e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Power Sources">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0038_R_d21364373e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Energy Mater.">38</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0047_R_d21364373e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Eng. J.">47</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0049_R_d21364373e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Sci.">49</a>-<a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0054_R_d21364373e1280" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Chem. Commun.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is speculated that the proper Nb doping in the tunnel structure not only enhance the electrode kinetics but also improves its structural stability during repeated charge and discharge.</div>
            
            <p>To further understand the sodium storage mechanism in the NMO and NMO-2 cathodes, in situ XRD measurements were performed. As presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), most of the XRD reflections, including (140), (150), (160), and (350) from NMO and NMO-2 gradually shift towards higher 2θ angles during the initial charge process, indicating the lattice shrinkage induced by the weakened electrostatic repulsion between adjacent O atoms after Na<sup>+</sup> extraction. Subsequently, during the discharge process, these reflection peaks continuously shift towards the original positions and even the lower angles due to the excessive insertion of 0.22 Na<sup>+</sup>. Notably, the intensity of the (350) peak continues to decrease until charging to 3.2&nbsp;V, followed by the generation of a new (0 10 0) peak. The newly formed (0 10 0) peak merges back into (350) peak and completely disappears after discharging below 2.7&nbsp;V, suggesting a reversible phase transition during Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction. The introduction of Nb does not fundamentally alter the structural evolution during charge and discharge, which is in good agreement with the results of galvanostatic charge/discharge and CV. Notably, the enlarged XRD patterns show that the spilt of (350) peak in NMO-2 (0.79°) is smaller than that in NMO (0.89°). This can be attributed to the enlarged tunnel space in NMO-2 by Nb doping, resulting in suppressed structural variation on the framework during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404442-sec-0050-title">2.3 Density Functional Theory Calculations</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">To understand the influence of Nb<sup>5+</sup> doping on the tunnel structure, density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted. Considering the partial occupations of Na, Nb, and Mn atoms in NMO and NMO-2 structures derived by the XRD Rietveld refinements, 143 structures with possible cation orderings were generated using the supercell program.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0055_R_d21364373e1310" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Cheminformatics">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Coulomb energies were performed with 1&nbsp;×&nbsp;1&nbsp;×&nbsp;3 supercells for 54 formula units (f.u.) of NMO, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4a</a>. The lowest energy configuration was adopted as the supercell for the DFT calculations. Moreover, the ground state energy structure is used to compute optimized lattice parameters for NMO. These calculations are performed on the experimental lattice parameters a = 9.094&nbsp;Å, b = 26.476&nbsp;Å, c = 8.479&nbsp;Å for NMO. The optimal lattice parameters obtained from DFT calculations is little lower (2%) than experimental values (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the reliability of the structual model for NMO. To enhance the concordance between calculations and experimental values, simulations were conducted on the four existing Mn5 sites within the structure. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4b</a>, the total energies of the most stable NMO structure and the NMO-2 structures with four potential Nb-doped Mn5 sites were calculated and compared to confirm the most stable substitution site. Based on the comparison, the Mn5-1 Nb-doping sites were identified as the most stable doping sites. To estimate the influence of Nb doping on structural stability, binding energies based on the NMO and NMO-2 structures (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information) were calculated, which can be defined as:

               <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202404442-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/bb443c29-35e2-40b2-8d88-42ebb82a5964/adfm202404442-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404442:adfm202404442-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202404442-math-0005.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi>b</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>t</mi>
                                    <mi>o</mi>
                                    <mi>t</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi>M</mi>
                                    <mi>n</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi>O</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi>X</mi>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{E}_b}\ = \ {{E}_{tot}} - {{E}_{Mn}} - \ {{E}_O} - \ {{E}_X}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>E<sub>tot</sub></i> denotes the total energies of NMO and NMO-2, and <i>E<sub>Mn</sub></i>, E<sub><i>O</i></sub> and <i>E<sub>X</sub></i> denote the energies of the isolated Mn, O, and X (= Nb) atoms, respectively. The calculated binding energy of NMO-2 is −7.72&nbsp;eV, which is smaller than that of NMO (−7.68&nbsp;eV). It is worth mentioning that the binding energy is directly correlated to the interplanar bonding strength in structures and thus provides direct information on the improved thermodynamic stability of NMO-2 with Nb doping. Moreover, Mn<sup>3+</sup> ions in transition metal oxide materials are known as Jahn–Teller-active ions, leading to Mn dissolution and capacity fade during cycling with elongated Mn─O bond lengths.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0049_R_d21364373e1391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Adv. Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the local charge density and bond lengths of Mn<sup>3+</sup>-O near the Nb-doped site are illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4d</a>. When Nb<sup>5+</sup> is doped in NMO, the Mn<sup>3+</sup>-O long bond and short bond are shortened by 0.08 and 0.02&nbsp;Å, respectively, resulting in suppressed Jahn–Teller distortion and reduced average Mn─O bond length. To visualize more clearly the Mn<sup>3+</sup>─O bond character, the corresponding charge densities of NMO and NMO-2 structures are compared. It is illustrated that O atoms are polarized toward the Mn<sup>3+</sup> atoms after Nb doping, resulting in more charge aggregation regions in the Mn<sup>3+</sup>─O bonds. Therefore, NMO-2 possesses improved structural stability with enhanced confinement of Mn<sup>3+</sup> in MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra.</div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404442-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/44946529-82d8-4167-a171-ea1568027fa6/adfm202404442-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/44946529-82d8-4167-a171-ea1568027fa6/adfm202404442-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/9f28eded-8ff6-45b9-95af-9a56d85e28fa/adfm202404442-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/44946529-82d8-4167-a171-ea1568027fa6/adfm202404442-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404442-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404442" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Electrostatic energies of 143 NMO structures created by the supercell program.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0055" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0055_R_d21364373e1437" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Cheminformatics">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lowest energy configuration was marked by a triangle. b) Total energies with the corresponding crystal structures of the most stable NMO structure and the NMO-2 structure with four potential Nb-doped Mn5 sites. c) The binding energies of NMO and NMO-2 structures. d) Contour maps of total charge density distributions and local atomic structures in Mn–O planes of NMO and NMO-2. e) Density of States (DOS) for NMO and NMO-2 structures. f) UV–vis spectra of the NMO and NMO-2 samples. g) Na2-Na2 diffusion path between the initial and final sites represented by the yellow-white spheres in the unit cell of NMO-2. h) Computed diffusion barriers in NMO and NMO-2 structures.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">To clarify the influence of Nb doping on the electronic structure, the total density of states (TDOS) of NMO and NMO-2 structures are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4e</a>. It is noticed that both NMO and NMO-2 structures possess narrow band gaps, which agree well with the previous studies in the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0040_R_d21364373e1448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Chem. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4e</a>, the Fermi level moves up to the conduction band after Nb doping, indicating an enhanced electronic conductivity in NMO-2. Meanwhile, the UV–vis spectroscopy measurements in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4f</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information) were further performed to investigate bandgap evolution. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4f</a> illustrates that a smaller band gap of ≈0.16&nbsp;eV was achieved for NMO-2, while a ≈0.50&nbsp;eV band gap was found for NMO, which is consistent with the calculation results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4e</a>) and explains the reduced ohmic resistance for the NMO-2 electrode as illustrated in the EIS result (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7d</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0056_R_d21364373e1472" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Nanoscale">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To better understand the rate performance difference between NMO and NMO-2 electrodes, the climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB) method was employed to evaluate the energy barrier (Δ<i>E<sub>a</sub></i>) for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. In CI-NEB calculations, migration barrier energy of Na<sup>+</sup> ion hoping is calculated between initial and final states by considering linearly interpolated images as.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0057_R_d21364373e1486" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - NPG Asia Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202404442-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/41f54942-8d88-4394-b405-6ac99128d0ba/adfm202404442-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202404442:adfm202404442-math-0006" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202404442-math-0006.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi>max</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>E</mi>
                                 <mi>initial</mi>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\Delta {{{\textit{E}}}_{\mathrm{a}}} = {{{\textit{E}}}_{{\mathrm{max}}}} - {{{\textit{E}}}_{{\mathrm{initial}}}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>where Δ<i>E<sub>a</sub></i> is the migration barrier of saddle point, and <i>E<sub>max</sub></i> and <i>E<sub>initial</sub></i> are the maximum energy during the migration path and the optimized energy at the initial state for the hopping Na<sup>+</sup> ion, respectively. It is difficult to systematically consider all possible Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion pathways in a system like NMO with a lot of Na<sup>+</sup>-hopping sites. Therefore, CI-NEB calculations for NMO and NMO-2 structures were carried out for three selected representative pathways, as presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0004">4g,h</a> and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). Notably, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier of the Na2-to-Na2 path in NMO-2 was calculated to be 0.32&nbsp;eV, which is obviously lower than that in NMO (0.43&nbsp;eV). Therefore, the Nb doping can efficiently boost the Na<sup>+</sup> migration kinetics, which is in good accordance with the rate performance.</div>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202404442-sec-0060-title">2.4 Na Compensation for High–Efficiency Cathode</h3>
            
            <p>Considering the low ICE of NMO in SIBs, a sodium compensation strategy was proposed to enable the practical application of tunnel-structured cathode in SIBs. Here, layered structured NaCrO<sub>2</sub> powders were prepared via a simple solid-state reaction and used to compensate for the sodium-deficient tunnel-type NMO-2 cathode. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16a</a> (Supporting Information), the XRD pattern of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> powders can be well indexed to an O3-type layered structure (JCPDS card no. 88–0720). SEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16b,c</a>, Supporting Information) of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> powders reveal the irregular morphologies for the particles of ≈1&nbsp;µm in size. The charge/discharge behavior of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> electrode was first investigated as presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). At a current rate of C/10, the initial specific charge capacity is ≈252&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, while the initial discharge capacity is only 50&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. After the first cycle, little capacity contribution occurs in the subsequent charge and discharge processes, indicating the feasibility of using NaCrO<sub>2</sub> as a self-sacrificing sodium compensation additive. To further explore the effect of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> on the sodium compensation, the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 composite cathodes with various amounts of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> additive (0, 8, and 15 wt%) were prepared. The initial charge and discharge curves of different composite electrodes between 2 and 4.2&nbsp;V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information). The initial specific charge/discharge capacities (based on the total mass of NMO-2 and NaCrO<sub>2</sub>) of the composite cathodes at 0.1 C are 56.1/109.3, 77.6/105.15, and 97.7/101.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, a little decrease, owing to the reduced amount of NMO-2, in the discharge capacities was discovered, while the NMO-2 cathode was mixed with 0, 8, and 15 wt% NaCrO<sub>2</sub> additive, respectively. Meanwhile, the corresponding Coulombic efficiency as a function of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> content in the composite electrodes was plotted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0005">5a</a>. Obviously, the ICE of NMO-2 can be greatly improved when NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is added. Especially when the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> content reaches 15 wt%, the ICE of the composite electrode can achieve ≈100%. To further evaluate the applicability of this strategy, the electrochemical performances of the NMO-2 and NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 (15 wt% NaCrO<sub>2</sub>) cathodes were compared. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0005">5b</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) show the charge/discharge curves of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 and NMO-2 cathodes at a current rate of C/10. Apart from the first cycle, the charge and discharge curves of the NMO-2 and NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathodes show similar voltage plateaus, suggesting similar reaction mechanisms for both cathodes. However, the specific reversible capacity of NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 is slightly lower than that of NMO-2, which can be attributed to the low activity of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> component after the first cycle. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> (Supporting Information), the typical CV curve of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> within the voltage range of 2.0–4.2&nbsp;V only shows cathodic peaks but no detectable anodic peaks, demonstrating promising Na compensation behavior. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0005">5c,d</a> show the CV curves of the NMO-2 and NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathodes between 2 and 4.2&nbsp;V (vs. Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) for the initial two cycles. In comparison, the 2nd cycle CV curves for the two cathodes show similar redox peaks, while for the 1st cycle, the CV curve of NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 shows high potential cathodic peaks between 3.6 and 4.2&nbsp;V. These high potential cathodic peaks disappear in the second cycle, confirming the self-sacrificing Na contribution of NaCrO<sub>2</sub> additive in the composite cathode. Cycling tests were carried out for both NMO-2 and NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathodes at 5 C for 800 cycles. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0005">5e</a>, despite the lower specific capacity for the composite cathode, the capacity retention is slightly increased from 82.3% for NMO-2 to 88.0% for NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 after 800 cycles, suggesting NaCrO<sub>2</sub> additive is also beneficial for extending the cycle life of NMO-2. Moreover, both cathodes show a high Coulombic efficiency of ≈100% after initial cycles, indicating good reversibility between charge and discharge. These findings demonstrate that NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is a promising sodium compensation additive, which can effectively reduce the initial capacity loss for the tunnel-structured NMO without compromising its cycle performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202404442-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/72349178-899b-4d45-92fc-c4cdce673f1f/adfm202404442-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/72349178-899b-4d45-92fc-c4cdce673f1f/adfm202404442-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7f8eeabb-810b-46a9-97a8-9f8744444577/adfm202404442-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/72349178-899b-4d45-92fc-c4cdce673f1f/adfm202404442-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202404442-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202404442" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The ICE as a function of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> content for the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 composite cathode. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the b) NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathode at a current rate of C/10. CV curves of the c) NMO-2, and d) NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathodes at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. e) Cycle performances of the NMO and NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathodes at 5 C. f) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles at 0.1 C of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2||HC and NMO-2||HC pouch full cells. g) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of the NaCrO2/NMO-2||HC pouch full cell at different current densities. h) Cycle performance and the corresponding Coulombic efficiencies of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2||HC pouch full cell tested at 0.2 C.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To assess the practical applicability of adding the NaCrO<sub>2</sub> additive to the NMO-2 cathode, a pouch-type full cell was assembled using the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathode and hard carbon (HC) anode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0005">5f</a>, the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2||HC full cell exhibited an initial discharge capacity of 80.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, while only 49.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was delivered by the NMO-2||HC full cell, which clearly demonstrated the effective Na compensation by NaCrO<sub>2</sub> for the full cell. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0005">5g</a> displays the galvanostatic charge-discharge curves of the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2||HC full cell at different current densities from 0.1 to 2&nbsp;C, revealing good rate performance. Moreover, the 0.8 Ah full cell achieved a high energy density of 153&nbsp;wh&nbsp;kg<sup>−1</sup> (based on the total mass of active materials from the anode and cathode) and retained 75% of its initial capacity after 150 cycles at 0.2 C with nearly 100% coulombic efficiency (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202404442-fig-0005">5h</a>).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404442-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, the tunnel-structured NMO cathode material with a trace of Nb doping has been successfully synthesized via a simple solid-state reaction method. It is found that the proper amount of Nb-doping not only improves the rate capability but also prolongs the cycling lifespan of NMO. The NMO-2 cathode exhibits excellent rate performance (76.6% capacity retained from 50 to 3&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and superior cycling stability (82.3% capacity retention after 800 cycles). As disclosed from both the experimental and theoretical analysis, the introduction of Nb in the tunnel structure can induce favorable structural regulation, improving structural stability, electronic conductivity, and Na<sup>+</sup> transportation kinetics. Furthermore, NaCrO<sub>2</sub> is implemented as a self-sacrificing sodium compensation additive, which can effectively compensate for the deficient sodium in tunnel structure during the initial charge process and improve the ICE to near 100%. Therefore, the findings in the present work provide new insights into designing advanced manganese-based cathodes with high Coulombic efficiency, fast Na<sup>+</sup> storage kinetics, and long cycle life for future practical application in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404442-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202404442-sec-0110-title"> Material Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>Nb-doped tunnel-type Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> samples were prepared by a solid-state reaction method using the chemicals (Sigma–Aldrich) of Mn<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (99%), NaHCO<sub>3</sub> (99%), and Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> (99%). An excess of 5 moL% of NaHCO<sub>3</sub> was used to compensate for the sodium loss during the high-temperature sintering process. The starting materials were mixed through ball milling for 10&nbsp;h. Then, the obtained precursors with different Nb/Mn molar ratios of 0.005:1, 0.01:1, 0.02:1, and 0.03:1 were calcined at 600&nbsp;°C for 8&nbsp;h and 900&nbsp;°C for 10&nbsp;h in air. The obtained final products with different Nb-doping levels were correspondingly referred to as NMO-0.5, NMO-1, NMO-2, and NMO-3, respectively. In comparison, the pristine Na<sub>0.44</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> material (NMO) was synthesized by a similar route without adding Nb sources. NaCrO<sub>2</sub> was prepared via a simple solid-state reaction. Stoichiometric amounts of NaHCO<sub>3</sub> and Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> were mixed and ball-milled for 12&nbsp;h. The powdery sample was heated at 900&nbsp;°C under flowing argon for 5&nbsp;h. To fabricate the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 composite, NaCrO<sub>2</sub> was intimately blended with the as-prepared NMO-2 power according to the mass ratio of 8:92 and 15:85. When assembling the pouch-type cell, the NaCrO<sub>2</sub>/NMO-2 cathode and HC anode were coated on the carbon coated aluminum foil and aluminum foil current collectors, respectively. The active mass ratio of anode to cathode was carefully set at ≈1:1.1 to balance the capacity, and the voltage range for the cycling test was fixed at 1.0–3.95&nbsp;V.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202404442-sec-0120-title"> Material Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>The structure of the synthesized materials has been characterized by XRD (Bruker-D8) with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5406&nbsp;Å) in a 2<i>θ</i> range form 10 to 70°. The XRD spectra were further analyzed by Rietveld refinement using TOPAS software. The Na/Mn and Nb/Mn atomic ratios in synthesized samples were measured by ICP-OES (Agilent 5110). The morphologies and microstructures of the samples were characterized using SEM (Quant 250FEG) and TEM (JEM2100). EDS was carried out to provide qualitative elemental analysis, and XPS measurements were carried out by using a Thermo Fisher Nexsa system. UV–vis measurements were carried out using a PerkinElmer lambda-35 in transmission mode, at a wavelength of 200–800&nbsp;nm.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202404442-sec-0130-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>The cathode electrodes were prepared by dispersing active materials into N-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP) with Super P and poly(vinylidenefluoride) (PVDF) in a 70:20:10 wt.% ratio and then coated on an aluminum foil with mass loading of 2–3&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. The electrodes were oven-dried at 80&nbsp;°C under vacuum for 12&nbsp;h. For all electrochemical measurements, 1<span class="smallCaps">m</span>&nbsp;NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate (EC) and diethyl carbonate (DEC) (v/v = 1:1) solution was used as the electrolyte, and glass fiber was used as the separator. The materials were assembled in a coin cell (CR-2025) with metallic sodium as both counter electrode and anode. CV, Galvanostatic charge-discharge, and EIS measurements were carried out on a CHI660E electrochemical workstation. All the EIS tests were conducted over a frequency range from 10<sup>5</sup> to 0.01 Hz.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202404442-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202404442-sec-0140-title"> Theoretical Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>First-principles DFT calculations were performed under the generalized gradient approximation (GGA)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0058_R_d21364373e1838" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0059_R_d21364373e1843" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Phys. Rev. B">59</a>, <a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0060_R_d21364373e1843" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Comp. Mater. Sci.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Static electronic structure calculations were carried out with a 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;1&nbsp;×&nbsp;2 Monkhorst–Pack k-point grid<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0061_R_d21364373e1848" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Phys. Rev. B">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and an energy cutoff of 520&nbsp;eV for plane waves. Projector augmented wave (PAW) method<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0062_R_d21364373e1854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Phys. Rev. B">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was used to describe core-valence interactions. The HSE method was used to predict the total density of states (TDOS), which then led to an estimation of the electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0063_R_d21364373e1859" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Energy Environ. Sci.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The energy and force were converged to 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV and 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>−2</sup>&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>. Climbing image nudged elastic band (CI-NEB)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0064_R_d21364373e1871" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - J. Chem. Phys.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> calculations were performed to obtain Na<sup>+</sup>-ion migration energy based on static potential energy surface. A large number of structures are generated using the supercell program.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202404442-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202404442-bib-0055_R_d21364373e1879" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Cheminformatics">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202404442-sec-0150-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>

The authors thank the Materials Characterization Facility of Nanjing University of Science and Technology for XRD, FESEM, and UV measurements.</p>
